## APPENDIX B: BLOOD WORK REFERENCE RANGES FOR FITNESS CLIENTS

The following reference ranges represent optimal values for fitness-oriented clients, which may differ from standard laboratory reference ranges. Standard lab ranges are based on population averages that include sedentary and unhealthy individuals. The "optimal" ranges below reflect targets used in functional medicine and anti-aging practice for active adults.

**Important:** Blood work interpretation for medical decision-making is outside the fitness coach's scope of practice. Use this table to understand what markers are relevant and why, and to have informed conversations with clients and their physicians. Always defer to the client's physician for medical interpretation.

### B.1 Hormonal Markers

| Marker | Standard Lab Range | Optimal Range (Active Adults) | What It Tells You | Relevant Peptides | Notes |
|--------|-------------------|-------------------------------|-------------------|-------------------|-------|
| IGF-1 | 50-350 ng/mL (age-dependent) | 200-300 ng/mL (adults 25-50) | Reflects integrated GH status over 24h; marker for GH secretagogue efficacy | All GH secretagogues (sermorelin, CJC-1295, ipamorelin, GHRP-2, GHRP-6, hexarelin, MK-677), IGF-1 LR3 | Levels decline with age (~14% per decade after 30); test fasting AM; must monitor during GH secretagogue use |
| Total Testosterone (male) | 264-916 ng/dL | 600-900 ng/dL | Anabolic status, muscle protein synthesis capacity, recovery, libido, mood | Kisspeptin (may stimulate LH/T), MK-677 (mild indirect effect via GH/sleep); SARMs suppress T | Draw 7-10 AM fasting; serial measurements more informative than single |
| Free Testosterone (male) | 5-21 pg/mL | 15-25 pg/mL | Bioavailable testosterone; accounts for SHBG binding | Same as total T | Free T often more informative than total T; calculate via equilibrium dialysis, not analog assay |
| Total Testosterone (female) | 15-70 ng/dL | 30-60 ng/dL | Muscle maintenance, bone density, libido, energy | Kisspeptin | Female T is critical but under-monitored; test in follicular phase |
| Estradiol (E2, male) | 10-40 pg/mL | 20-35 pg/mL | Aromatase activity; too high = water retention, gynecomastia; too low = joint pain, libido issues | GH secretagogues may slightly influence via GH/IGF-1 interactions | Context-dependent: optimal E2 depends on total T level |
| Estradiol (E2, female) | Varies by cycle phase | Phase-dependent; 30-400 pg/mL | Ovarian function, bone density, cardiovascular protection | Kisspeptin may influence cycle | Test relative to menstrual cycle phase |
| SHBG | 10-57 nmol/L (male); 18-144 nmol/L (female) | 20-40 nmol/L (male); 40-80 nmol/L (female) | Binds testosterone/estradiol; affects free hormone levels | Indirectly affected by insulin/metabolic status (GLP-1 agonists may increase SHBG via weight loss) | Low SHBG associated with insulin resistance; high SHBG reduces free T |
| DHEA-S | 80-560 mcg/dL (male, age-dependent); 35-430 mcg/dL (female, age-dependent) | Upper half of age-adjusted range | Adrenal androgen precursor; marker of adrenal reserve and biological aging | Indirectly affected by overall hormonal optimization | Declines ~2-3% per year after age 25 |
| Cortisol (AM) | 6-23 mcg/dL | 10-18 mcg/dL (AM draw) | Stress response, adrenal function, recovery capacity | GHRP-2 and GHRP-6 acutely raise cortisol; MK-677 minimal effect; ipamorelin has no cortisol effect | 4-point salivary cortisol provides better diurnal pattern than single AM serum |
| Prolactin | 2-18 ng/mL (male); 2-29 ng/mL (female) | Lower half of range | Elevated levels can indicate pituitary issues, suppress testosterone | GHRP-2 and GHRP-6 may transiently raise prolactin; hexarelin raises it significantly; ipamorelin does not | Chronically elevated prolactin requires medical evaluation |
| TSH | 0.45-4.5 mIU/L | 0.5-2.5 mIU/L | Thyroid function screening; affects metabolism, body composition, energy | Not directly affected by most peptides, but thyroid function impacts response to all protocols | Optimal TSH is lower than the standard range upper limit |
| Free T3 | 2.0-4.4 pg/mL | 3.0-4.0 pg/mL | Active thyroid hormone; drives metabolic rate | GH secretagogues may increase T4-to-T3 conversion | More metabolically informative than TSH alone |
| Free T4 | 0.8-1.7 ng/dL | 1.0-1.5 ng/dL | Thyroid hormone precursor; converted to active T3 | As above | Should be interpreted alongside Free T3 and TSH |

### B.2 Metabolic Markers

| Marker | Standard Lab Range | Optimal Range (Active Adults) | What It Tells You | Relevant Peptides | Notes |
|--------|-------------------|-------------------------------|-------------------|-------------------|-------|
| Fasting Glucose | 65-99 mg/dL | 75-90 mg/dL | Glycemic control; monitors for insulin resistance and GH-related glucose effects | MK-677, GH secretagogues (may elevate); semaglutide, tirzepatide (lower it); MOTS-c (improves insulin sensitivity) | MUST monitor during GH secretagogue use; GH is anti-insulinogenic |
| Fasting Insulin | 2.6-24.9 uIU/mL | 3-8 uIU/mL | Insulin sensitivity; early marker of metabolic dysfunction; precedes glucose elevation | Semaglutide/tirzepatide (improve); MK-677 (may worsen); MOTS-c (improves) | Calculate HOMA-IR: (glucose x insulin) / 405; optimal < 1.5 |
| HbA1c | 4.0-5.6% | 4.5-5.2% | 3-month average glucose; more stable than fasting glucose | Same as fasting glucose | Gold standard for long-term glycemic monitoring during peptide protocols |
| HOMA-IR (calculated) | < 2.5 (standard) | < 1.5 | Insulin resistance index | All metabolic peptides | (Fasting glucose mg/dL x fasting insulin uIU/mL) / 405 |
| Total Cholesterol | 125-200 mg/dL | 150-200 mg/dL | Lipid metabolism overview | GLP-1 agonists generally improve lipid profiles; GH optimization can affect lipids | Interpret in context of HDL/LDL ratio and particle size |
| LDL-C | < 130 mg/dL (standard) | < 100 mg/dL; < 70 mg/dL if high CVD risk | Atherogenic lipoprotein | Semaglutide/tirzepatide modestly reduce LDL; GH normalization may affect | Consider advanced testing (LDL-P, apoB) for fuller picture |
| HDL-C | > 40 mg/dL (male); > 50 mg/dL (female) | > 50 mg/dL (male); > 60 mg/dL (female) | Atheroprotective lipoprotein; increased by exercise | Weight loss (via GLP-1 agonists) may transiently lower HDL; exercise and body comp improvements raise it | Exercise is the most potent HDL raiser |
| Triglycerides | < 150 mg/dL | < 100 mg/dL; ideally < 80 mg/dL | Metabolic health marker; elevated = insulin resistance, excess carbs, alcohol | Semaglutide/tirzepatide significantly reduce; GH secretagogues may modestly reduce | Must be fasting (12h); carb and alcohol intake strongly influence |
| apoB | < 130 mg/dL (standard) | < 90 mg/dL; < 60 mg/dL if high risk | Best single marker of atherogenic particle burden; 1:1 with LDL particle number | GLP-1 agonists reduce | Superior to LDL-C for cardiovascular risk assessment |
| Lp(a) | < 30 mg/dL (generally) | As low as possible; genetically determined | Independent CVD risk factor; largely genetic; resistant to lifestyle modification | Not significantly affected by peptides discussed in this book | Test once (genetic); if elevated, warrants cardiology consultation |

### B.3 Liver and Kidney Function

| Marker | Standard Lab Range | Optimal Range (Active Adults) | What It Tells You | Relevant Peptides | Notes |
|--------|-------------------|-------------------------------|-------------------|-------------------|-------|
| ALT (SGPT) | 7-56 U/L | < 30 U/L | Liver cell damage marker; can be mildly elevated from intense training | Monitor during oral peptide/compound use (MK-677, SARMs, 5-Amino-1MQ) | Muscle damage from training can transiently elevate; interpret with context |
| AST (SGOT) | 10-40 U/L | < 30 U/L | Liver and muscle cell damage; less liver-specific than ALT | Same as ALT | Significantly elevated by intense resistance training; wait 48-72h post-training for accurate liver assessment |
| GGT | 9-48 U/L | < 25 U/L | Liver enzyme; sensitive to alcohol, medications, biliary obstruction | Monitor during any hepatically-metabolized compound | Most sensitive liver enzyme for detecting metabolic stress |
| Creatinine | 0.7-1.3 mg/dL (male); 0.6-1.1 mg/dL (female) | Stable within personal range | Kidney function; affected by muscle mass (higher in muscular individuals) | Generally not directly affected; high-protein diets (recommended with GLP-1 agonists) may mildly elevate | Muscular clients may have "high" creatinine that is normal for them; track trend over time |
| eGFR | > 60 mL/min/1.73m2 | > 90 mL/min/1.73m2 | Estimated kidney filtration rate | Not directly affected; important baseline before any protocol | May underestimate kidney function in highly muscular individuals |
| BUN | 6-20 mg/dL | 10-18 mg/dL | Protein metabolism and kidney function | High-protein diets increase BUN; not a concern unless kidney function is impaired | Elevated BUN with normal creatinine usually reflects high protein intake, not kidney disease |

### B.4 Inflammatory and Immune Markers

| Marker | Standard Lab Range | Optimal Range (Active Adults) | What It Tells You | Relevant Peptides | Notes |
|--------|-------------------|-------------------------------|-------------------|-------------------|-------|
| hs-CRP | < 3.0 mg/L (standard) | < 1.0 mg/L; ideally < 0.5 mg/L | Systemic inflammation; independent CVD risk marker | BPC-157, TB-500, KPV (anti-inflammatory); semaglutide (reduces); thymosin alpha-1 (immunomodulatory) | Transiently elevated after intense training, illness, injury; test during non-inflamed state |
| ESR | 0-22 mm/hr (male); 0-29 mm/hr (female) | < 10 mm/hr | Non-specific inflammation marker; slower to rise/fall than CRP | Same as hs-CRP | Less specific than CRP; useful when chronic inflammation is suspected |
| Ferritin | 12-300 ng/mL (male); 12-150 ng/mL (female) | 50-150 ng/mL (male); 40-100 ng/mL (female) | Iron stores; also an acute-phase reactant (rises with inflammation) | Not directly affected by peptides | Interpret in context of CRP (if CRP is elevated, ferritin may be falsely elevated); low ferritin = iron deficiency |
| Homocysteine | 5-15 umol/L | < 8 umol/L | Methylation status; CVD risk; affected by B-vitamin status | Not directly affected | Elevated = possible B12/folate deficiency, increased CVD risk; easy to correct with methylated B-vitamins |
| WBC | 4,500-11,000 cells/mcL | 4,500-7,500 cells/mcL | Immune system activity; general health marker | Thymosin alpha-1 (immunomodulatory); LL-37 (antimicrobial) | Chronic elevation suggests ongoing infection or inflammation; chronic depression may indicate immune suppression |
| Vitamin D (25-OH) | 30-100 ng/mL | 50-80 ng/mL | Immune function, bone health, muscle function, hormone production | Not a peptide but critical for optimizing response to all protocols | Supplementation almost always needed; recheck 3 months after dose change |

---

## APPENDIX C: RECONSTITUTION AND DOSING CALCULATOR

### C.1 Understanding Peptide Forms

Most injectable peptides are supplied as lyophilized (freeze-dried) powder in sealed vials. Before use, the powder must be reconstituted (dissolved) with a sterile solvent, typically bacteriostatic water (BAC water) containing 0.9% benzyl alcohol as a preservative.

**Key terms:**

- **Lyophilized:** Freeze-dried to remove water; produces a white or off-white powder cake or pellet in the vial
- **Bacteriostatic water (BAC water):** Sterile water with 0.9% benzyl alcohol preservative; standard reconstitution solvent; allows multi-use over ~28 days
- **Sterile water:** Water without preservative; must be used within 24 hours of reconstitution; single-use
- **Reconstitution:** The process of dissolving lyophilized peptide into solution for injection

### C.2 Step-by-Step Reconstitution Guide

**Materials needed:**
1. Lyophilized peptide vial
2. Bacteriostatic water vial
3. Insulin syringe (1 mL / 100 units) or appropriate syringe
4. Alcohol swabs
5. Clean, well-lit workspace

**Procedure:**

**Step 1:** Remove the plastic cap from both the peptide vial and the BAC water vial. Wipe the rubber stopper of each vial with a fresh alcohol swab. Allow to air dry for 10-15 seconds.

**Step 2:** Draw the desired volume of BAC water into the syringe. The amount you add determines the concentration (see concentration table below).

**Step 3:** Insert the needle into the peptide vial at an angle, piercing the rubber stopper. Slowly dispense the BAC water down the inside wall of the vial. Do NOT spray the water directly onto the lyophilized powder, as this can damage the peptide and cause foaming.

**Step 4:** Allow the water to gently dissolve the powder. Do NOT shake the vial. Gently swirl or roll the vial between your palms if needed. Most peptides dissolve within 1-5 minutes.

**Step 5:** Once fully dissolved, the solution should be clear and free of particles. If the solution is cloudy or contains visible particles, do not use it.

**Step 6:** Store the reconstituted vial in the refrigerator (36-46 degrees F / 2-8 degrees C). Use within 28 days when reconstituted with BAC water.

### C.3 Concentration and Dosing Math

The fundamental equation:

```
Concentration = Total peptide in vial / Volume of solvent added
```

**Example 1: BPC-157 (5 mg vial)**
- Vial contains: 5 mg (5,000 mcg) of BPC-157
- You add: 2 mL of BAC water
- Concentration: 5,000 mcg / 2 mL = 2,500 mcg per mL
- Since 1 mL = 100 units on an insulin syringe:
- Each unit (tick mark) = 2,500 / 100 = 25 mcg
- For a 250 mcg dose: 250 / 25 = 10 units on the insulin syringe

**Example 2: Ipamorelin (5 mg vial)**
- Vial contains: 5 mg (5,000 mcg)
- You add: 2.5 mL of BAC water
- Concentration: 5,000 / 2.5 = 2,000 mcg per mL
- Each unit on insulin syringe = 2,000 / 100 = 20 mcg
- For a 200 mcg dose: 200 / 20 = 10 units
- For a 300 mcg dose: 300 / 20 = 15 units

**Example 3: CJC-1295 no DAC (2 mg vial)**
- Vial contains: 2 mg (2,000 mcg)
- You add: 2 mL of BAC water
- Concentration: 2,000 / 2 = 1,000 mcg per mL
- Each unit on insulin syringe = 1,000 / 100 = 10 mcg
- For a 100 mcg dose: 100 / 10 = 10 units

**Example 4: Semaglutide (compounded 5 mg vial, for reference)**
- Vial contains: 5 mg (5,000 mcg)
- Pre-mixed at 5 mg / 3 mL by pharmacy (common)
- Concentration: 1,667 mcg per mL
- For a 250 mcg dose (0.25 mg): 250 / 1667 = 0.15 mL = 15 units

### C.4 Quick Reference Concentration Table

| Peptide Amount in Vial | BAC Water Added | Concentration per mL | Concentration per Unit (insulin syringe) |
|------------------------|-----------------|---------------------|----------------------------------------|
| 2 mg (2,000 mcg) | 1 mL | 2,000 mcg/mL | 20 mcg/unit |
| 2 mg (2,000 mcg) | 2 mL | 1,000 mcg/mL | 10 mcg/unit |
| 5 mg (5,000 mcg) | 1 mL | 5,000 mcg/mL | 50 mcg/unit |
| 5 mg (5,000 mcg) | 2 mL | 2,500 mcg/mL | 25 mcg/unit |
| 5 mg (5,000 mcg) | 2.5 mL | 2,000 mcg/mL | 20 mcg/unit |
| 5 mg (5,000 mcg) | 5 mL | 1,000 mcg/mL | 10 mcg/unit |
| 10 mg (10,000 mcg) | 2 mL | 5,000 mcg/mL | 50 mcg/unit |
| 10 mg (10,000 mcg) | 5 mL | 2,000 mcg/mL | 20 mcg/unit |
| 10 mg (10,000 mcg) | 10 mL | 1,000 mcg/mL | 10 mcg/unit |

### C.5 Insulin Syringe Unit Guide

Standard insulin syringes are calibrated in "units" where 100 units = 1 mL.

- **1/2 mL (50 unit) syringe:** Each small tick = 1 unit. Best for small doses requiring precision.
- **1 mL (100 unit) syringe:** Each small tick = 2 units. Standard for most peptide doses.

Reading the syringe:
- 5 units = 0.05 mL
- 10 units = 0.10 mL
- 20 units = 0.20 mL
- 50 units = 0.50 mL
- 100 units = 1.00 mL

### C.6 Conversion Table

| From | To | Conversion |
|------|----|------------|
| 1 mg | mcg | 1,000 mcg |
| 1 mcg | mg | 0.001 mg |
| 1 mL | Units (insulin syringe) | 100 units |
| 1 unit (insulin syringe) | mL | 0.01 mL |
| 1 IU of GH | mg | ~0.33 mg (varies by manufacturer; 1 mg GH is approximately 3 IU) |
| 1 mg of GH | IU | ~3 IU (approximate) |

**Note on IU (International Units):** IU is used for growth hormone and some other biological substances. The conversion between IU and mg/mcg depends on the specific substance and its bioactivity. For recombinant hGH, 1 mg is approximately equal to 3 IU. This conversion does NOT apply to peptides like ipamorelin or CJC-1295, which are dosed in mcg.

### C.7 How Many Doses Per Vial?

```
Number of doses = Total peptide in vial / Dose per injection
```

**Example: BPC-157, 5 mg vial, 250 mcg per dose**
- 5,000 mcg / 250 mcg = 20 doses per vial

**Example: Ipamorelin, 5 mg vial, 300 mcg per dose, 2x daily**
- 5,000 mcg / 300 mcg = 16.7 doses per vial
- At 2 injections per day = approximately 8 days per vial

**Example: CJC-1295 no DAC, 2 mg vial, 100 mcg per dose, 1x daily**
- 2,000 mcg / 100 mcg = 20 doses per vial
- At 1 injection per day = 20 days per vial

---

## APPENDIX D: PEPTIDE INTERACTION MATRIX

The following matrix shows known and theoretical interactions between commonly stacked peptides. Interactions are classified as:

- **Synergistic (+):** Compounds enhance each other's effects through complementary mechanisms
- **Additive (=):** Compounds work independently without significant interaction
- **Caution (!):** Potential for amplified side effects or antagonistic mechanisms; use with monitoring
- **Contraindicated (X):** Should not be combined; significant risk of adverse interaction
- **Unknown (?):** Insufficient data to characterize the interaction

### D.1 Growth Hormone Secretagogue Combinations

| | CJC-1295 (no DAC) | CJC-1295 (DAC) | Ipamorelin | GHRP-2 | GHRP-6 | MK-677 | Hexarelin |
|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|
| **CJC-1295 (no DAC)** | -- | X (redundant; do not combine GHRH analogs) | + (classic combo: GHRH + GHRP synergy) | + (synergy, but GHRP-2 raises cortisol) | + (synergy, but GHRP-6 raises cortisol and hunger) | ! (redundant GH stimulation; increased glucose risk) | + (synergy, but watch tachyphylaxis and prolactin) |
| **CJC-1295 (DAC)** | X | -- | ! (DAC provides continuous GHRH; adding pulsatile GHRP is debated) | ! | ! | ! (excessive/continuous GH stimulation) | ! |
| **Ipamorelin** | + | ! | -- | = (both GHRPs; redundant, pick one) | = (redundant) | ! (both act on GHS-R1a) | = (redundant) |
| **GHRP-2** | + | ! | = | -- | = (redundant) | ! | = |
| **GHRP-6** | + | ! | = | = | -- | ! | = |
| **MK-677** | ! | ! | ! | ! | ! | -- | ! |
| **Hexarelin** | + | ! | = | = | = | ! | -- |

### D.2 Healing Peptide Combinations

| | BPC-157 | TB-500 | GHK-Cu | Collagen Peptides |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| **BPC-157** | -- | + (complementary mechanisms; widely stacked for injury recovery) | = (different targets; no known interaction) | = (oral collagen + injectable BPC is common) |
| **TB-500** | + | -- | = | = |
| **GHK-Cu** | = | = | -- | = |
| **Collagen Peptides** | = | = | = | -- |

### D.3 GH Secretagogues with Other Peptide Categories

| GH Secretagogues with: | Interaction | Notes |
|------------------------|-------------|-------|
| BPC-157 | = (additive) | No known negative interaction; GH may enhance healing synergistically |
| TB-500 | = (additive) | Both promote tissue repair through different mechanisms |
| Semaglutide | ! (caution) | GH secretagogues may raise fasting glucose; semaglutide lowers it; monitor glucose closely; competing metabolic effects |
| Tirzepatide | ! (caution) | Same concerns as semaglutide; GH's anti-insulin effects vs. GLP-1/GIP's insulin-sensitizing effects |
| AOD-9604 | = (additive) | AOD is a GH fragment; no direct GHS-R1a interaction |
| MOTS-c | = (additive) | MOTS-c improves insulin sensitivity, which may offset GH secretagogue glucose effects |
| Thymosin Alpha-1 | = (additive) | No known negative interaction; different target systems |
| Selank/Semax | = (additive) | Different target systems; no known interaction |
| Melanotan II | = (additive) | Different receptor systems; commonly co-used without reported issues |

### D.4 GLP-1 Agonist Interactions

| GLP-1 Agonists with: | Interaction | Notes |
|----------------------|-------------|-------|
| Semaglutide + Tirzepatide | X (contraindicated) | Never combine two GLP-1 agonists; severe GI side effects, hypoglycemia risk |
| GH Secretagogues | ! (caution) | Opposing effects on glucose metabolism; monitor fasting glucose and HbA1c |
| BPC-157 | = (additive) | BPC-157 has gastroprotective properties that may theoretically help with GLP-1 GI side effects (speculative) |
| Collagen Peptides | = (additive) | No interaction; collagen may support joint health during rapid weight loss |
| SARMs | ! (caution) | SARMs may help preserve lean mass during GLP-1-induced weight loss, but liver monitoring essential; both have hepatic effects |
| Creatine (non-peptide) | = (additive) | Creatine supplementation may help preserve lean mass; no pharmacological interaction |

### D.5 Important Combination Warnings

| Combination | Warning Level | Reason |
|-------------|--------------|--------|
| Multiple GH secretagogues simultaneously | Caution | Excessive GH stimulation; increased risk of insulin resistance, water retention, carpal tunnel, joint pain |
| GH secretagogues + exogenous GH | Caution | Redundant and potentially excessive; no benefit over one or the other; increased side effect risk |
| Multiple GHRPs (e.g., ipamorelin + GHRP-6) | Unnecessary | Redundant mechanism; choose one based on side effect profile preference |
| Semaglutide + liraglutide | Contraindicated | Never combine GLP-1 agonists |
| MK-677 + any other GH secretagogue | Caution | MK-677 already provides continuous GHS-R1a stimulation; adding pulsatile GHRPs may cause receptor desensitization |
| Follistatin 344 + IGF-1 LR3 | Unknown/Caution | Both potently anabolic; theoretical risk of excessive growth signaling; no human combination data |
| Any peptide + active cancer history | Medical supervision required | Most growth-promoting peptides (GH secretagogues, IGF-1, follistatin) are theoretically contraindicated in patients with cancer history |

---

## APPENDIX E: GLOSSARY OF TERMS

**Actin** — A globular protein that forms microfilaments in the cell cytoskeleton. TB-500 sequesters G-actin monomers to promote cell migration and wound healing.

**ADME** — Abbreviation for Absorption, Distribution, Metabolism, Excretion. The four stages of pharmacokinetics that determine how a drug moves through the body.

**Agonist** — A molecule that binds to a receptor and activates it, producing a biological response. Semaglutide is a GLP-1 receptor agonist.

**Allometric Scaling** — A method of adjusting drug doses between species based on body surface area rather than body weight. Used to estimate human-equivalent doses from animal studies.

**AMPK (AMP-Activated Protein Kinase)** — A cellular energy sensor enzyme that is activated when cellular energy is low. MOTS-c activates AMPK. AMPK activation promotes fat oxidation, glucose uptake, and mitochondrial biogenesis.

**Anabolic** — Pertaining to the building up of complex molecules from simpler ones. Anabolic processes include muscle protein synthesis, bone formation, and tissue growth.

**Antagonist** — A molecule that binds to a receptor and blocks its activation, preventing the biological response. Somatostatin is a GH release antagonist.

**Autophagy** — A cellular process in which damaged organelles and proteins are degraded and recycled. Fasting and AMPK activation promote autophagy. Relevant to longevity peptide mechanisms.

**Bacteriostatic Water (BAC Water)** — Sterile water containing 0.9% benzyl alcohol as a preservative. The standard solvent for reconstituting lyophilized peptides. Allows multi-dose vial use for up to 28 days.

**Bioavailability** — The fraction of an administered dose that reaches systemic circulation in its active form. Subcutaneous peptide injection typically has 60-80% bioavailability. Oral peptide bioavailability is often below 1% without special formulation.

**Bioidentical** — Structurally identical to the molecule produced by the human body. Sermorelin is bioidentical to the first 29 amino acids of endogenous GHRH.

**BDNF (Brain-Derived Neurotrophic Factor)** — A protein that supports the growth, differentiation, and survival of neurons. Semax and selank may increase BDNF expression.

**Catabolism** — The breakdown of complex molecules into simpler ones, releasing energy. Excessive cortisol promotes muscle catabolism.

**Certificate of Analysis (COA)** — A document from a third-party laboratory verifying the purity, potency, sterility, and composition of a peptide product.

**Compounding Pharmacy** — A pharmacy licensed to prepare customized medications based on individual prescriptions. 503A pharmacies compound for individual patients; 503B outsourcing facilities compound larger batches.

**Cytokine** — A small signaling protein involved in immune and inflammatory responses. Pro-inflammatory cytokines include TNF-alpha, IL-1beta, and IL-6. Anti-inflammatory peptides like BPC-157 and KPV modulate cytokine expression.

**DAC (Drug Affinity Complex)** — A chemical modification that enables a peptide to bind to serum albumin, dramatically extending its half-life. CJC-1295 with DAC has a half-life of approximately 8 days versus approximately 30 minutes without DAC.

**Desensitization (Tachyphylaxis)** — A reduction in receptor responsiveness following prolonged or repeated stimulation. Hexarelin is particularly prone to GHS-R1a desensitization. Many GH secretagogue protocols include cycling to mitigate desensitization.

**Diurnal** — Relating to a 24-hour cycle. Growth hormone secretion follows a diurnal pattern with the largest pulse occurring during deep sleep.

**DPP-4 (Dipeptidyl Peptidase-4)** — An enzyme that rapidly degrades native GLP-1 and GIP. GLP-1 agonists like semaglutide are engineered to resist DPP-4 degradation.

**Endogenous** — Originating within the body. Endogenous GH is the growth hormone your body produces naturally.

**Exogenous** — Originating outside the body. Exogenous peptide administration introduces peptide molecules from an external source.

**FAK-Paxillin Pathway** — A cellular signaling pathway involved in cell adhesion, migration, and tissue repair. BPC-157 is reported to activate this pathway, which may contribute to its wound-healing effects.

**Fibroblast** — A cell type responsible for producing collagen and other components of the extracellular matrix. GHK-Cu and BPC-157 stimulate fibroblast activity.

**GHS-R1a (Growth Hormone Secretagogue Receptor 1a)** — The receptor for ghrelin and synthetic growth hormone-releasing peptides (GHRPs). Also known as the ghrelin receptor. Expressed in the pituitary, hypothalamus, and other tissues.

**GHRH (Growth Hormone-Releasing Hormone)** — A 44-amino-acid peptide hormone produced by the hypothalamus that stimulates GH release from pituitary somatotrophs. Sermorelin, CJC-1295, and tesamorelin are GHRH analogs.

**GHRP (Growth Hormone-Releasing Peptide)** — Synthetic peptides that stimulate GH release via the GHS-R1a (ghrelin) receptor. Includes GHRP-2, GHRP-6, ipamorelin, and hexarelin.

**GLP-1 (Glucagon-Like Peptide-1)** — An incretin hormone produced by intestinal L-cells that stimulates insulin secretion, suppresses glucagon, slows gastric emptying, and reduces appetite. Semaglutide, tirzepatide, and liraglutide are GLP-1 receptor agonists.

**GIP (Glucose-Dependent Insulinotropic Polypeptide)** — An incretin hormone that enhances insulin secretion. Tirzepatide is a dual GIP/GLP-1 receptor agonist.

**Half-Life** — The time required for the concentration of a substance in the body to decrease by 50%. Determines dosing frequency. Native GLP-1 has a half-life of approximately 2 minutes; semaglutide has a half-life of approximately 7 days.

**HOMA-IR (Homeostatic Model Assessment of Insulin Resistance)** — A calculated index of insulin resistance using fasting glucose and fasting insulin: (glucose x insulin) / 405. Values below 1.5 indicate good insulin sensitivity.

**HPA Axis (Hypothalamic-Pituitary-Adrenal Axis)** — The neuroendocrine system governing cortisol production and the stress response. Some GHRPs (GHRP-2, GHRP-6) stimulate the HPA axis, transiently elevating cortisol.

**HPG Axis (Hypothalamic-Pituitary-Gonadal Axis)** — The neuroendocrine system governing testosterone and estrogen production. Kisspeptin is a master regulator of the HPG axis.

**IGF-1 (Insulin-Like Growth Factor 1)** — A peptide hormone produced primarily by the liver in response to GH stimulation. Mediates many of GH's anabolic and growth-promoting effects. Serum IGF-1 is the primary monitoring marker for GH secretagogue protocols.

**IGFBP (Insulin-Like Growth Factor Binding Protein)** — A family of proteins that bind IGF-1 and IGF-2 in circulation, modulating their bioavailability and activity. IGF-1 LR3 has reduced IGFBP binding, increasing its bioactive free fraction.

**In Vitro** — Experiments conducted outside a living organism, typically in cell cultures or test tubes. Latin for "in glass."

**In Vivo** — Experiments conducted in a living organism. Latin for "in the living."

**Intramuscular (IM)** — Administration by injection into muscle tissue. Some peptides (e.g., MGF for local muscle effects) are administered IM.

**Intranasal** — Administration via the nasal mucosa. Selank and semax are commonly administered intranasally.

**Lipidation** — Chemical modification of a peptide by attaching a fatty acid chain. Semaglutide uses C18 fatty acid lipidation to bind albumin, extending its half-life.

**Lyophilized** — Freeze-dried. Peptides are commonly supplied in lyophilized form for stability during storage. Must be reconstituted before use.

**MC4R (Melanocortin-4 Receptor)** — A receptor in the central nervous system involved in appetite regulation, energy homeostasis, and sexual function. PT-141/bremelanotide activates MC4R.

**Melanocortin** — A family of peptide hormones derived from proopiomelanocortin (POMC) that activate melanocortin receptors (MC1R through MC5R). Melanotan II is a synthetic melanocortin analog.

**mTOR (Mechanistic Target of Rapamycin)** — A kinase that serves as a master regulator of cell growth, protein synthesis, and autophagy. IGF-1 and insulin activate mTOR. The mTOR pathway is central to muscle hypertrophy.

**Myostatin (GDF-8)** — A protein that acts as a negative regulator of muscle growth. Follistatin binds and neutralizes myostatin, removing the brake on muscle hypertrophy.

**NAD+ (Nicotinamide Adenine Dinucleotide)** — A coenzyme essential for cellular energy metabolism, DNA repair, and sirtuin activation. NAD+ levels decline with age. NMN and NR are precursors that boost NAD+ levels.

**Negative Feedback** — A regulatory mechanism in which the output of a system inhibits its own production. IGF-1 exerts negative feedback on GH secretion via the hypothalamus and pituitary.

**NF-kB (Nuclear Factor Kappa-B)** — A transcription factor that plays a central role in the inflammatory response. KPV and BPC-157 are reported to inhibit NF-kB activation.

**Off-Label Use** — The use of an FDA-approved drug for an indication, age group, dosage, or route of administration that is not specified in the approved labeling. Tesamorelin prescribed for non-HIV visceral fat reduction is off-label use.

**PEGylation** — The covalent attachment of polyethylene glycol (PEG) chains to a peptide or protein to increase its stability, reduce immunogenicity, and extend its half-life. PEG-MGF is PEGylated mechano growth factor.

**Pharmacodynamics** — The study of what a drug does to the body: its mechanism of action, therapeutic effects, and side effects.

**Pharmacokinetics** — The study of what the body does to a drug: absorption, distribution, metabolism, and excretion (ADME).

**Proteolysis** — The enzymatic breakdown of proteins and peptides into smaller fragments or amino acids. Proteolysis limits the oral bioavailability of most peptides.

**Pulsatility** — The pattern of hormone release in discrete bursts (pulses) rather than continuous secretion. Growth hormone is released in a pulsatile pattern, with the largest pulse during deep sleep. GHRH analogs and GHRPs promote pulsatile GH release, while CJC-1295 with DAC produces a more continuous (blunted pulsatility) pattern.

**Receptor** — A protein molecule on the surface of or within a cell that binds to a specific molecule (ligand) and initiates a cellular response.

**Satellite Cell** — A type of muscle stem cell that resides between the basal lamina and sarcolemma of muscle fibers. When activated by mechanical stress or growth factors (including MGF and IGF-1), satellite cells proliferate and fuse with existing muscle fibers to support repair and hypertrophy.

**Senescence (Cellular)** — A state in which cells permanently cease dividing but remain metabolically active and secrete pro-inflammatory factors (the senescence-associated secretory phenotype, or SASP). FOXO4-DRI targets senescent cells for apoptosis.

**Senolytic** — An agent that selectively kills senescent cells. FOXO4-DRI is a senolytic peptide.

**SARM (Selective Androgen Receptor Modulator)** — A class of non-steroidal compounds that selectively activate androgen receptors in muscle and bone with reduced activity in prostate and skin. Not peptides, but frequently discussed alongside them.

**Somatotroph** — A cell type in the anterior pituitary gland that produces and secretes growth hormone. GHRH and GHRPs act on somatotrophs to stimulate GH release.

**Somatostatin** — A peptide hormone produced by the hypothalamus (and other tissues) that inhibits GH release. Somatostatin acts as the "brake" in the GH regulatory system. GH secretagogue protocols often involve timing around somatostatin troughs to maximize GH pulse amplitude.

**Subcutaneous (SubQ)** — Administration by injection into the tissue layer between the skin and muscle. The most common route for peptide administration. Injection sites include abdominal fat, thigh, and upper arm.

**Tachyphylaxis** — Rapid decrease in the response to a drug after repeated doses over a short period. Hexarelin demonstrates significant tachyphylaxis of GH release. Also known as acute tolerance or desensitization.

**Telomerase** — An enzyme that adds telomeric DNA repeats to the ends of chromosomes, counteracting telomere shortening. Epithalon reportedly activates telomerase.

**Telomere** — Repetitive DNA sequences (TTAGGG in humans) at the ends of chromosomes that protect genomic integrity. Telomeres shorten with each cell division and are considered a biomarker of biological aging.

**TGF-beta (Transforming Growth Factor Beta)** — A superfamily of signaling proteins involved in cell growth, differentiation, and tissue repair. Myostatin (GDF-8) is a member of the TGF-beta superfamily.

**Titration** — The process of gradually adjusting a dose upward (or downward) to find the optimal therapeutic dose while minimizing side effects. GLP-1 agonists require slow upward titration to manage GI side effects.

**VEGF (Vascular Endothelial Growth Factor)** — A signaling protein that promotes angiogenesis (new blood vessel formation). BPC-157 is reported to upregulate VEGF, which may contribute to its tissue repair effects.

**WADA (World Anti-Doping Agency)** — The international organization that maintains the prohibited substances list for competitive sport. Many peptides discussed in this textbook, including all GH secretagogues and IGF-1 variants, are prohibited by WADA.

---

## APPENDIX F: RECOMMENDED READING AND RESOURCES

### F.1 Key Textbooks

- **Goodman & Gilman's The Pharmacological Basis of Therapeutics** (14th Edition, 2023). The gold standard pharmacology reference. Chapters on pituitary hormones, GLP-1, and peptide pharmacology are directly relevant.
- **Lehninger Principles of Biochemistry** (8th Edition, Nelson & Cox). Essential for understanding amino acid chemistry, protein structure, signal transduction, and metabolic pathways.
- **Williams Textbook of Endocrinology** (14th Edition, Melmed et al.). Comprehensive endocrinology reference. Chapters on GH axis, hypothalamic-pituitary regulation, and hormone action.
- **Guyton and Hall Textbook of Medical Physiology** (14th Edition). Accessible physiology reference for understanding hormonal feedback loops, metabolism, and tissue repair.
- **Peptide Drug Discovery and Development** (Castanho & Santos, Wiley). Technical reference on peptide drug design, pharmacokinetics, and formulation.
- **Exercise Physiology: Nutrition, Energy, and Human Performance** (McArdle, Katch, and Katch). The classic exercise physiology textbook; covers hormonal responses to exercise relevant to peptide optimization.

### F.2 Key Researchers to Follow

- **Predrag Sikiric, MD, PhD** (University of Zagreb): Leading BPC-157 researcher; over 200 publications on gastric pentadecapeptides.
- **Vladimir Khavinson, PhD** (St. Petersburg Institute of Bioregulation and Gerontology): Pioneer in bioregulatory peptides including epithalon/epitalon.
- **Cyril Bowers, PhD** (Tulane University): Discovered growth hormone-releasing peptides (GHRPs); foundational GHS-R1a research.
- **Jeffrey Bhatt, MD / Michelle Ionescu, PhD**: CJC-1295 clinical research.
- **Stanley Goldspink, PhD** (University College London): Discoverer of mechano growth factor (MGF).
- **John Eng, MD** (Bronx VA Medical Center): Discovered exendin-4 in Gila monster venom, leading to the entire GLP-1 agonist drug class.
- **Loren Pickart, PhD**: Pioneer in GHK-Cu (copper peptide) research.
- **Jens Juul Holst, MD, DMSc** (University of Copenhagen): Foundational GLP-1 physiology research.
- **Daniel Rudman, MD**: Published the landmark 1990 NEJM paper on GH replacement in aging men that launched the anti-aging GH movement.
- **Ana Maria Cuervo, MD, PhD** (Albert Einstein College of Medicine): Autophagy and longevity research relevant to peptide-informed aging interventions.
- **David Sinclair, PhD** (Harvard Medical School): NAD+ biology and longevity; relevant to NMN/NR context.

### F.3 Peer-Reviewed Journals

- **Journal of Clinical Endocrinology & Metabolism (JCEM)**: The primary journal for endocrine research including GH secretagogues, IGF-1, and hormonal axis studies.
- **New England Journal of Medicine (NEJM)**: Published the STEP trials (semaglutide), SURMOUNT trials (tirzepatide), and other landmark peptide research.
- **The Lancet**: Published key GLP-1 agonist trials and peptide safety studies.
- **Cell Metabolism**: Publishes foundational research on MOTS-c, AMPK, mTOR, and metabolic peptide mechanisms.
- **American Journal of Clinical Nutrition (AJCN)**: Relevant for collagen peptide supplementation studies and nutrition-peptide interactions.
- **Nature Medicine**: High-impact peptide translational research.
- **Endocrine Reviews**: Comprehensive review articles on hormonal systems relevant to peptide therapy.
- **British Journal of Sports Medicine (BJSM)**: Relevant for healing peptide research in athletic contexts.
- **Growth Hormone & IGF Research**: Specialized journal for GH axis research.
- **Current Pharmaceutical Design**: Frequently publishes BPC-157 and peptide mechanism reviews.
- **Obesity**: Published key weight management trials relevant to GLP-1 agonists and AOD-9604.
- **Aging Cell**: Relevant for epithalon, senolytic, and longevity peptide research.

### F.4 Research Databases and Tools

- **PubMed** (pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov): The essential database for searching biomedical literature. Use MeSH terms for more precise searches.
- **ClinicalTrials.gov**: Registry of ongoing and completed clinical trials. Search for active peptide trials to stay informed about emerging evidence.
- **Examine.com**: Evidence-based supplement and compound analysis with graded evidence summaries. Covers several peptides and related compounds.
- **Google Scholar** (scholar.google.com): Broader search than PubMed; includes preprints and conference proceedings.
- **Sci-Hub**: (Access to paywalled research; legality varies by jurisdiction. Mentioned for awareness.)
- **Cochrane Library**: Gold standard for systematic reviews and meta-analyses.

### F.5 Podcasts and Educational Media

- **Huberman Lab** (Andrew Huberman, PhD): Detailed neuroscience-based episodes on GH optimization, peptide mechanisms, sleep, and recovery.
- **The Drive** (Peter Attia, MD): Longevity-focused discussions including peptide therapy, metabolic health, and evidence evaluation.
- **The Peptide Podcast** (Dr. Seeds): Focused specifically on peptide science and clinical applications.
- **Joe Rogan Experience**: Occasional peptide discussions with guests; brought peptides to mainstream awareness.
- **FoundMyFitness** (Rhonda Patrick, PhD): Molecular mechanisms relevant to longevity peptides, NAD+ biology, and metabolic health.
- **Mind Pump**: Fitness industry perspective on peptides; practical coaching implications.

### F.6 Professional Organizations and Continuing Education

- **American Academy of Anti-Aging Medicine (A4M)**: Conferences and certifications relevant to peptide-informed practice.
- **Age Management Medicine Group (AMMG)**: Clinical education in anti-aging medicine including peptide therapy.
- **International Peptide Society (IPS)**: Professional organization focused on peptide therapy education.
- **National Strength and Conditioning Association (NSCA)**: While not peptide-specific, maintaining S&C credentials demonstrates professional competence.

### F.7 Accessing Full-Text Research Articles

For fitness professionals who want to read full research papers rather than just abstracts:

- **PubMed Central (PMC)**: Free full-text archive of biomedical literature. Many papers are available here after an embargo period.
- **University library access**: If affiliated with a university (as a student, alumni, or community member), you may have access to institutional journal subscriptions.
- **Interlibrary loan**: Many public libraries can obtain specific articles through interlibrary loan at no cost.
- **Author requests**: Many researchers will share their papers if you email them directly. This is common and generally welcomed.
- **Open access journals**: Increasing numbers of journals (PLOS ONE, Frontiers series, BMC series) publish all articles as open access.

---

### G.2 Peptide Contraindication Screening Checklist

**PEPTIDE CONTRAINDICATION SCREENING**

*This checklist is for the fitness coach's reference when discussing peptide-informed programming with clients. It does NOT replace medical screening by a licensed provider.*

**Absolute Contraindications (Refer to Physician Before Proceeding):**

[ ] Active cancer or malignancy of any type
[ ] History of cancer within the past 5 years (especially for GH secretagogues and IGF-1)
[ ] Pregnancy or planning to become pregnant
[ ] Currently breastfeeding/nursing
[ ] Active diabetic retinopathy (risk with GH-related peptides)
[ ] Uncontrolled diabetes (HbA1c > 9%)
[ ] Active pituitary tumor or history of pituitary adenoma
[ ] Known allergy to any peptide component
[ ] Severe renal impairment (eGFR < 30)
[ ] Severe hepatic impairment
[ ] Personal or family history of medullary thyroid carcinoma (contraindication for GLP-1 agonists)
[ ] Personal or family history of MEN2 syndrome (contraindication for GLP-1 agonists)
[ ] History of pancreatitis (relative contraindication for GLP-1 agonists)

**Relative Contraindications (Require Medical Clearance and Monitoring):**

[ ] Prediabetes or insulin resistance (monitor glucose with GH secretagogues)
[ ] History of carpal tunnel syndrome (GH-related peptides may exacerbate)
[ ] Edema or fluid retention conditions
[ ] History of intracranial hypertension
[ ] Hypothyroidism (GH secretagogues may unmask or exacerbate)
[ ] Scoliosis (in younger clients using GH-related peptides)
[ ] History of gastroparesis (GLP-1 agonists worsen gastric emptying)
[ ] History of gallbladder disease (GLP-1 agonists increase gallstone risk)
[ ] Currently taking blood thinners (injection-related bleeding)
[ ] History of eating disorder (GLP-1 agonist appetite suppression may complicate)
[ ] Age under 25 (GH axis still maturing; most peptides inappropriate)
[ ] Age over 65 (increased sensitivity to side effects; lower starting doses)
[ ] Competitive athlete subject to WADA testing (many peptides are prohibited)

---

### G.3 Informed Consent Template (Peptide Education Disclaimer)

**INFORMED CONSENT FOR PEPTIDE-INFORMED FITNESS COACHING**

*Please read this document carefully and ask any questions before signing.*

**Client Name:** _______________
**Coach Name:** William Marceau, Fitness Coach & Peptide Expert
**Date:** _______________

**1. NATURE OF SERVICES**

I understand that William Marceau is a fitness professional who incorporates peptide science education into fitness coaching services. These services include exercise programming, nutrition guidance, recovery strategies, body composition tracking, and education about peptide science as it relates to fitness and body composition.

**2. SCOPE OF PRACTICE ACKNOWLEDGMENT**

I understand and acknowledge that:

a) My coach is a fitness professional, NOT a medical doctor, pharmacist, nurse practitioner, or licensed prescriber of any kind.

b) My coach does NOT prescribe, sell, administer, or recommend specific peptides for specific medical conditions. My coach provides education about peptide science and adjusts fitness programming to complement protocols prescribed by my physician.

c) Any information my coach provides about peptides is educational in nature and based on publicly available scientific literature. It is NOT medical advice, diagnosis, or treatment.

d) If I choose to use any peptide, I will do so only under the supervision of a licensed healthcare provider who has evaluated my individual health status and determined that the peptide is appropriate for me.

**3. RISKS AND LIMITATIONS**

I understand that:

a) Many peptides discussed in educational contexts are not FDA-approved for the uses described, and their long-term safety profiles may not be fully established.

b) Peptide science is a rapidly evolving field, and current understanding may change as new research emerges.

c) Individual responses to peptides vary significantly, and no specific outcome can be guaranteed.

d) Fitness coaching, with or without peptide knowledge, carries inherent risks including but not limited to musculoskeletal injury, cardiovascular events, and other health complications.

**4. MY RESPONSIBILITIES**

I agree to:

a) Consult with a licensed physician before beginning or modifying any peptide protocol.

b) Provide my coach with accurate and complete health history information.

c) Inform my coach of any changes in my health status, medications, or peptide protocols.

d) Communicate any adverse reactions, symptoms, or concerns promptly to both my coach and my physician.

e) Not hold my coach responsible for any decisions I make regarding peptide use.

**5. CONFIDENTIALITY**

I understand that my health information will be kept confidential and will not be shared with third parties without my written consent, except as required by law.

**6. CONSENT**

By signing below, I acknowledge that I have read and understood this document, have had the opportunity to ask questions, and voluntarily consent to receive peptide-informed fitness coaching services.

Client Signature: _______________ Date: _______________

Printed Name: _______________

Coach Signature: _______________ Date: _______________
